# Cluster Prose Extract — M06: Hate, Contempt and Hostility

_Generated: 2026-05-07_

_Cluster status_: **Analysis Completed**

## At a glance

- Active terms (mti_terms): **34**
- Anchor verses (is_anchor=1, active): **57**
- Findings (cluster_finding, non-empty): **1516**
- Findings by tier: T0=97, T1=194, T2=249, T3=264, T4=192, T5=168, T6=192, T7=160

### Sub-groups

| Code | Label | Definition |
|---|---|---|
| M06-A | Hatred | Sustained inner hostility directed at a person. |
| M06-B | Contempt | Inner posture of regarding as beneath regard. |
| M06-C | Abhorrence | Visceral moral revulsion from evil or the repugnant. |
| M06-D | Cruelty/Ruthlessness | Character disposition of merciless destructiveness. |
| M06-E | Reproach | Experienced condition of public contempt and shame. |
| M06-F | Hostility/Enmity | Relational posture of structured opposition. |
| M06-G | Malice | Compound inner disposition: hostility + contempt + soul-level pleasure in another's destruction. |
| BOUNDARY | Boundary/expression | Edge-of-scope characteristics — expressive acts, quality terms, character disorder, or metaphorical usage; retained in M06 with explicit boundary marker, not internally sub-grouped. |

When the human person hates what God hates, the verses treat this as alignment with God, not as moral failure. Righteous hatred is therefore not merely a permitted human response but a participation in the divine inner life. |

Whether person-directed hatred existed in any form before the fall is not directly addressed by the verses. |

| 49 | M06-A | T0.3.1 | In what way does this characteristic express the divine image in the human person — what aspect of … | finding | Hatred of evil expresses the divine image through moral alignment — hating what God hates, in the same direction and for the same reasons. The image-bearing function of righteous hatred is its role as moral discernment made into an orientation: not just knowing that evil is wrong, but being constitutionally set against it. |

This is not analogy but participation: the human person takes on God's own disposition as an act of alignment. |

Job 34:17: "shall one who hates justice govern?" — hatred of justice is the disqualifying inner disposition, the sign that the image is disordered at its governing level. |

Since human beings are made in God's image, and contempt is absent from God's originating character, contempt is a distortion of the image — an inversion of the reverence and honour-giving that belongs to the properly ordered creature. |

Whether contempt could theoretically have a righteous form (appropriate disregard for what is genuinely worthless) is not addressed by the verse evidence. The corpus treats contempt as a characteristically fallen disposition. |

| 50 | M06-B | T0.3.1 | In what way does this characteristic express the divine image in the human person — what aspect of … | finding | Contempt distorts rather than expresses the divine image. The image of God in the human person involves the capacity for accurate moral evaluation — assigning worth in accordance with what things actually are. Contempt inverts this: it assigns low worth to what has high worth (God's name, covenant, word). The capacity for worth-evaluation exists in the image-bearer; contempt is that capacity running in the wrong direction. |
| 58 | M06-B | T0.3.2 | Does the evidence suggest that this characteristic is shared between God and the human person, or i… | finding | Not shared as an originating disposition. Contempt of the sacred marks a person in whom the image's capacity for appropriate reverence and honour-giving has been inverted. The image is in disorder when the person despises what God values. |

| 3 | M06-C | T0.1.1 | What does the verse evidence reveal about the nature or character of God that this characteristic r… | finding | God is extensively the subject of abhorrence. The ta.av root carries physical resonance — the sense of something that cannot be tolerated in one's presence. God's abhorrence reveals that he is not indifferent to evil but constitutionally repelled by it at the level of his inner being. When the righteous person abhors falsehood (Psa 119:163), they are participating in God's own inner-being reality. |

When the human person shares this abhorrence (Psa 119:163), they are expressing the divine image. |
| 19 | M06-C | T0.1.3 | Where Scripture is silent about God's possession of this characteristic, what does that silence sug… | finding | Not applicable in the same sense — ta.av is extensively attributed to God. The created purpose of abhorrence is to mark what is genuinely repugnant to God's character and to maintain the inner being's separation from it. |

| 20 | M06-D | T0.1.3 | Where Scripture is silent about God's possession of this characteristic, what does that silence sug… | finding | The silence is total for cruelty as an inner-being characteristic. Cruelty's absence from God's nature means it cannot be an expression of the divine image; it can only be its negation. |

| 52 | M06-D | T0.3.1 | In what way does this characteristic express the divine image in the human person — what aspect of … | finding | Cruelty expresses the negation of the divine image. Lam 4:3: "even jackals offer the breast; they nurse their young; but the daughter of my people has become cruel, like the ostriches in the wilderness." The comparison to ostriches — creatures who abandon their young — names cruelty as the failure of the most basic nurturing impulse. Even animals express what the cruel person has lost. The divine image includes mercy, restraint, and care for the vulnerable; cruelty is the condition of having lost these. |
| 60 | M06-D | T0.3.2 | Does the evidence suggest that this characteristic is shared between God and the human person, or i… | finding | Not shared. Cruelty is the inner-being condition produced by the removal of God-consciousness from the person's operational centre. |

| 13 | M06-E | T0.1.2 | Does Scripture explicitly attribute this characteristic to God — and if so, what does that attribut… | finding | Not as a held characteristic. God imposes the condition covenantally (Eze 5:14) but does not himself inhabit the condition of being reproached as an inner-being state. The significance for the human person: reproach is a condition God takes seriously enough to act upon — both imposing it (as judgment) and removing it (as restoration). It matters to God. |
| 21 | M06-E | T0.1.3 | Where Scripture is silent about God's possession of this characteristic, what does that silence sug… | finding | God does not inhabit the condition of reproach as one who is reproached (he imposes and removes it). The silence on God experiencing reproach as an inner-social condition suggests that reproach belongs to the creaturely register — it is a condition that arises in the relational vulnerability of creatures among creatures, and in the covenantal context of faithfulness and unfaithfulness. It is not a condition of the divine being as such, though Christ's bearing of it (Psa 69:9 → Rom 15:3) is the redemptive exception. |
| 29 | M06-E | T0.2.1 | What does the verse evidence suggest about the purpose this characteristic serves in the human pers… | finding | Reproach does not serve a created purpose in the sense of a positive inner capacity. It is a condition imposed from outside — the experience of being an object of public contempt and shame. Unlike righteous hatred or abhorrence, there is no commanded righteous reproach. Reproach is what happens to the person, not what they exercise. |
| 37 | M06-E | T0.2.2 | Does the evidence indicate whether this characteristic is part of the original created design, a re… | finding | Fallen condition only. Reproach arises from the disordered inner lives of those who hate, despise, and mistreat others (M06-A through D). In a world without disordered inner lives, there would be no reproach. |
| 45 | M06-E | T0.2.3 | Is there evidence that this characteristic is oriented toward a future fullness — something the per… | finding | The future orientation is complete removal and restoration of honour. Isa 25:8: "the reproach of his people he will take away from all the earth." Isa 54:4: "the reproach of your widowhood you will remember no more." The eschatological future for reproach is not its completion but its total removal followed by permanent honour. |
| 53 | M06-E | T0.3.1 | In what way does this characteristic express the divine image in the human person — what aspect of … | finding | Reproach attacks the divine image in the person who bears it. Psa 22:6: "I am a worm and not a man, scorned by mankind and despised by the people" — the reproach has reduced the person, in their own experience and in the social register, to sub-human status. God's removal of reproach is therefore an act of image-restoration — the reinstatement of the honour that properly belongs to the image-bearer. |
| 61 | M06-E | T0.3.2 | Does the evidence suggest that this characteristic is shared between God and the human person, or i… | finding | Not shared as a condition experienced. God removes reproach; he does not inhabit it. Christ's bearing of reproach (Psa 69:9 → Rom 15:3) is the redemptive exception — the Son entering the creaturely condition of being reproached in order to remove it. |
| 69 | M06-E | T0.3.3 | What does the presence or absence of this characteristic in a person reveal about the condition of … | finding | For the one bearing reproach: the divine image in the bearer is not destroyed by reproach but assaulted, hidden, and temporarily eclipsed. The person can remain aligned with God even while their social status is destroyed — Psa 69:7: "for it is for your sake that I have borne reproach." For those imposing reproach: their contempt and hatred (M06-B, M06-A) reveal their image-disorder. God's restoration of honour to the reproached is an image-restoration act. |
| 77 | M06-E | T0.4.1 | Does Scripture use this characteristic typologically — deploying it to point toward or participate … | finding | Yes. Psa 69:9: "the reproaches of those who reproach you have fallen on me" — cited in Rom 15:3 as fulfilled in Christ. The one who bears the reproaches directed at God becomes the central typological pattern of M06-E. Christ bears what the righteous sufferer of Psa 69 bore, but in ultimate and cosmic scope. |
| 85 | M06-E | T0.4.2 | If typological use is present, what is the direction of the typology — does the human instance poin… | finding | Human to messianic fulfilment. The psalmist's experience of bearing reproach for God's sake (Psa 69) points forward to Christ who bears those very reproaches on behalf of all (Rom 15:3). The direction runs from the human suffering-for-God instance to the messianic bearing-on-behalf-of-all. |
| 93 | M06-E | T0.4.3 | If no typological use is evidenced, note this explicitly. | finding | [Sub-group not separately addressed in source — see cluster-level finding (cluster_subgroup_id IS NULL) for this prompt] |
| 6 | M06-F | T0.1.1 | What does the verse evidence reveal about the nature or character of God that this characteristic r… | finding | Exo 23:22 is decisive: "I will be an enemy (a.yav) to your enemies and an adversary to your adversaries." God explicitly adopts the adversary posture on Israel's behalf. This reveals that the adversary structure is not intrinsically wrong — God can take it up. What makes an adversary relationship godly or ungodly is not the structure of opposition but what is being opposed and why. God's adversary posture is covenantal, responsive, and purposive — adopted in service of his people's protection. |
| 14 | M06-F | T0.1.2 | Does Scripture explicitly attribute this characteristic to God — and if so, what does that attribut… | finding | Yes, explicitly in Exo 23:22. The attribution reveals that the adversary posture — structural opposition in service of covenant faithfulness — can be a righteous and even divine act. This makes M06-F unique among the cluster: it is the sub-group where the structural form (the adversary relationship) is shared with God in a positive sense. |
| 22 | M06-F | T0.1.3 | Where Scripture is silent about God's possession of this characteristic, what does that silence sug… | finding | The silence on God inhabiting a perpetual state of enmity toward his creation suggests that the adversary posture, while God can take it up (Exo 23:22), is not his natural orientation toward his creatures. His originating orientation is love and covenant; the adversary posture is adopted in response to specific conditions, not as a baseline. |
| 30 | M06-F | T0.2.1 | What does the verse evidence suggest about the purpose this characteristic serves in the human pers… | finding | The adversary relationship serves the purpose of justice and covenant advocacy. Exo 23:22 shows God adopting the adversary posture in service of covenant protection. The created purpose of the adversary-facing structure is not the hostility itself but the relational structure that creates the context for appeal, justice, and covenant vindication. |
| 38 | M06-F | T0.2.2 | Does the evidence indicate whether this characteristic is part of the original created design, a re… | finding | Response to the fallen condition. God's adoption of the adversary posture (Exo 23:22) is responsive to the existence of enemies, not part of creation's original design. The adversary structure arises in a world where opposition exists. |
| 46 | M06-F | T0.2.3 | Is there evidence that this characteristic is oriented toward a future fullness — something the per… | finding | The adversary structure moves toward its own defeat. Job 22:20: "our adversaries are cut off." 1Pe 5:8's cosmic adversary has a terminus. The eschatological trajectory is the elimination of all adversarial opposition. |
| 54 | M06-F | T0.3.1 | In what way does this characteristic express the divine image in the human person — what aspect of … | finding | The capacity to stand in structured opposition on behalf of justice reflects the divine image as God exercises it in Exo 23:22. The image is expressed through the capacity for advocacy — taking up the adversary posture when covenant faithfulness requires it, on behalf of those who cannot contend for themselves. |
| 62 | M06-F | T0.3.2 | Does the evidence suggest that this characteristic is shared between God and the human person, or i… | finding | Shared in the specific form of the adversary posture adopted in covenant faithfulness (Exo 23:22). God takes up the adversary relationship on behalf of his people. The capacity to stand in structured opposition as an act of advocacy is therefore shared — but only in its covenantal form, not in its hostile or adversarial form as directed against the person. |
| 70 | M06-F | T0.3.3 | What does the presence or absence of this characteristic in a person reveal about the condition of … | finding | The capacity to stand in appeal before God when facing an adversary (Psa 35:1, Jer 15:15, Luk 18:3) reveals an image-bearing person who recognises their dependence and God's authority over all adversarial structures. The absence of this response — facing opposition without appeal — would indicate a form of self-sufficiency inconsistent with the creature's proper orientation. |
| 78 | M06-F | T0.4.1 | Does Scripture use this characteristic typologically — deploying it to point toward or participate … | finding | Yes. 1Pe 5:8's naming of the devil as antidikos (adversary/accuser) places M06-F in the cosmic spiritual-conflict register. | 86 | M06-F | T0.4.2 | If typological use is present, what is the direction of the typology — does the human instance poin… | finding | Human-legal to cosmic-spiritual. Malice is the compound of enmity, contempt, and soul-level pleasure in another's destruction. None of these, in their compound form, is a characteristic God possesses. The absence is absolute and significant. |

| 55 | M06-G | T0.3.1 | In what way does this characteristic express the divine image in the human person — what aspect of … | finding | Malice is the most complete inversion of image-bearing joy in M06. Malice inverts this: it is the soul's rejoicing in destruction, which is the most complete inversion of the image-bearing capacity for joy. The soul was made to rejoice in life; malice rejoices in its ending. |

All three constitutive elements of malice (enmity, contempt, pleasure in destruction) represent inversions of image-bearing capacities. --- ### T0.4 — Typological Significance |

| 97 | M06-A | T1.1.1 | What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about it… | finding | Called "Hatred." The name signals a sustained inner orientation — not a momentary reaction but a settled disposition of the person turned against something or someone. It signals duration and direction: the person is *against*, and they remain so. |
| 105 | M06-A | T1.1.2 | What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical… | finding | Five terms: - **H8130 sa.ne** — the primary verb "to hate," carrying the sense of sustained hostility. The grammatical range (active, passive, agent-noun, infinitive construct) encodes hatred as a condition, a defining characteristic of a person-type, and a legally operative prior state. The most attested term (139 active verses). - **H8135 sin.ah** — the noun form "hating/hatred as a condition" — hatred as a thing that subsists in a person or relationship, with causal force (Pro 10:12: "hatred stirs up strife"). - **H7852 sa.tam** — persistent grudge-bearing hatred that waits, smoulders, and… |
| 113 | M06-A | T1.1.3 | Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry… | finding | Directional and relational. All five terms are inherently relational — they require an object. The direction of the hatred is its defining feature. Constitutional implication: Lev 19:17 locates it "in your heart"; 2Sa 5:8 locates it "in David's soul." The characteristic is at home in both heart and soul. |
| 121 | M06-A | T1.2.1 | What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, … | finding | Primarily a **disposition** — a settled, durable inner orientation. Gen 37:5 shows hatred intensifying over time. Gen 27:41 shows sa.tam settling and persisting. Pro 10:12 treats sin.ah as a force that "stirs up strife" — acting from within as ongoing pressure. Also has a **condition** form (sa.ni — being hated as a lived condition) and an **act** form (the momentary triggering). The dominant kind across the corpus is disposition. |
| 129 | M06-A | T1.2.2 | Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first … | finding | Has constituent elements: (1) triggering perception of injury or injustice; (2) inner settlement of the hostile disposition; (3) surface manifestation in changed behaviour (inability to speak peacefully); (4) intensification with new provocations; (5) concealment or expression; (6) volitional organisation toward action. |
| 137 | M06-A | T1.2.3 | What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutio… | finding | Hatred (M06-A) is a settled inner disposition of the person oriented against another person, quality, or reality — constitutionally located in the heart and soul, engaging the relational and volitional faculties, producing either sustained enmity (when directed at persons) or moral alignment (when directed at evil in the pattern of God's own hatred). It is inherently relational and directional, always requiring an object, intensifying when not resolved, and concealing itself when expression is dangerous. |
| 145 | M06-A | T1.3.1 | What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands agains… | finding | Love (a.ha.vah). Pro 10:12: "hatred stirs up strife, but love covers all offences." 2Sa 13:15: "the hatred with which he hated her was greater than the love with which he had loved her." Ecc 9:6: "their love and their hate...have already perished." The pairing is explicit and structural throughout. The opposite is not the absence of hatred but the positive disposition of love. |
| 153 | M06-A | T1.3.2 | What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist? | finding | In person-directed form (group 1601): peaceful speech (Gen 37:4 — "could not speak peacefully to him"), forgiveness and reconciliation, covenantal solidarity. In hatred of discipline (Pro 12:1, 15:10): growth, correction, and formation. The characteristic resists reconciliation — the verses show no pathway from hatred to restoration that does not first pass through the cessation of the hatred. |
| 161 | M06-A | T1.3.3 | What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin? | finding | Anger is not hatred. Hatred is settled; anger can be momentary. Gen 27:41 shows sa.tam (settled hatred) persisting after the heat of anger has passed — the grudge remains when the emotion dissipates. Deu 19:6 distinguishes judicial killing from hateful murder by the absence of prior hatred: "he had not hated his neighbour in the past." Contempt (M06-B) is adjacent: contempt is a worth-evaluation (treating as beneath regard), hatred is relational hostility (being against). One can despise without hating; one can hate without despising. |
| 169 | M06-A | T1.4.1 | In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person? | finding | Four modes: (1) **Concealment mode** — hatred operates within while the person presents a false exterior (Pro 10:18: "the one who conceals hatred has lying lips"; Pro 26:24: "whoever hates disguises himself with his lips and harbours deceit in his heart"). (2) **Accumulation mode** — hatred intensifies when fed by new provocations (Gen 37:5, 8: "they hated him even more"). (3) **Settlement mode** — sa.tam: the hatred settles into a plan (Gen 27:41: Esau says internally "then I will kill my brother Jacob"). (4) **Expression mode** — hatred breaks through into visible behaviour when restraint f… |
| 177 | M06-A | T1.4.2 | Does this characteristic operate differently depending on context, direction, or constitutional lev… | finding | Significantly. When directed at evil (group 550-NEW-02): hatred operates as moral discernment in expression — it stabilises the inner orientation toward truth and against falsehood. When directed at persons (group 1601): hatred operates as relational enmity — isolating, concealing, corrosive. When hatred of God (group 3596): hatred operates as a constitutive orientation that defines the person's entire covenantal standing. When communal-atmospheric (group 1199 — mas.te.mah): hatred operates as a shared inner climate that destroys collective spiritual capacity. |
| 185 | M06-A | T1.4.3 | Does this characteristic have a communicative or speech-based mode of operation — and if so, how do… | finding | Yes. Pro 10:18: "the one who conceals hatred has lying lips" — speech as the instrument of concealment. Psa 109:3: "they encircle me with words of hate" — speech as the vehicle of hostile expression. Zec 8:17: "love no false oath, for all these things I hate" — God's hatred of false speech shows that covenant-faithful speech is what hatred of deception produces. The characteristic operates through speech in both its concealed (deceptive) and expressed (hostile) forms. |
| 193 | M06-A | T1.5.1 | What is the first or most immediate inner-being response to receiving or encountering this characte… | finding | From the person who is hated: Psa 69:20: "reproaches have broken my heart, so that I am in despair. I looked for pity, but there was none." The immediate inner response to being hated is a shattering of the inner self — heart-breaking — followed by the cry for pity. From the one who receives a triggering injury and begins to hate: settlement of a hostile inner posture (Gen 37:4 — hatred immediate upon perceiving favouritism). |
| 201 | M06-A | T1.5.2 | Is the immediate response consistent across the verse evidence, or does it vary? | finding | Consistent in direction: hatred consistently produces rupture or closure. The specific form varies — acute (Gen 37:4 — immediate inability to speak peacefully) or sustained (Gen 27:41 — immediate internal murderous resolution) — but the pattern of inner breaking or inner organisation around a hostile intention is consistent. |
| 209 | M06-A | T1.5.3 | Where the verse evidence is silent on immediate response, note this explicitly. | finding | The process by which hatred takes hold internally — how it moves from triggering perception to settled disposition — is not described in the verses. |
| 217 | M06-A | T1.6.1 | What does this characteristic produce in the inner being over time? | finding | In the hater: progressive narrowing of the inner life around the object of hatred. Pro 10:18: concealed hatred requires lies — the person becomes complicit in self-deception. Hos 9:7: accumulated communal hatred produces "the prophet is a fool; the man of the spirit is mad" — the spiritual perceptive capacity is obliterated by the accumulated inner climate. In the hated: sustained hatred from others produces despair (Psa 69:20), fear of abandonment (Gen 50:15), and the chronic condition of disfavour (group 550-NEW-01 — the unloved wife across Deuteronomy and Genesis). |
| 225 | M06-A | T1.6.2 | What states, qualities, capacities, or orientations does sustained exposure to or possession of thi… | finding | Readiness for retributive action (Gen 50:15 — anticipated). | 241 | M06-A | T1.7.1 | What inner conditions or orientations enable genuine reception of this characteristic? | finding | Perceived injustice or loss (Gen 37:4 — brothers' perception of favouritism). Because of the wickedness of their deeds"). |
| 249 | M06-A | T1.7.2 | What inner conditions block, distort, or prevent reception? | finding | Love as the primary blocking condition: Pro 10:12 — "love covers all offences" — what could become the seed of hatred is covered rather than nursed. Lev 19:17's command to frank confrontation prevents the inner settlement of hatred by keeping the relational channel open. |
| 257 | M06-A | T1.7.3 | What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic but does not receive… | finding | Job 31:29: "if I have rejoiced at the ruin of him who hated me" — the righteous person does not move into reciprocal hatred when hated. Exo 23:5: "if you see the donkey of one who hates you lying down under its burden, you shall rescue it with him" — the command assumes it is possible to act for the benefit of the person who hates you without reciprocating. The inner state is not described directly but implied: the person maintains a different inner-being orientation — grounded in something beyond the relational exchange — that enables acting for the enemy's good. |
| 265 | M06-A | T1.8.1 | What is the primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic from the programme's dimension voc… | finding | **Relational Disposition.** Hatred is constitutively relational — it always requires an object, always involves the positioning of the self against another. Every group in M06-A involves a relationship: person against person, spouse against spouse, person against evil (in alignment with God's relational stance), God's relational stance toward sin. |
| 273 | M06-A | T1.8.2 | What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification? | finding | Every group in M06-A involves a relationship: person against person (1601), spouse against spouse (550-NEW-01), person against evil in alignment with God (550-NEW-02), God's relational stance toward sin (3595, 3596). |

| 98 | M06-B | T1.1.1 | What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about it… | finding | Called "Contempt." The name signals an evaluative act — the inner posture of treating something as beneath regard. It signals downward assessment: the person renders a verdict of low or no worth against its object. |
| 106 | M06-B | T1.1.2 | What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical… | finding | Six terms across distinct registers: - **H5006 na.ats** — the dominant term (25 verses); spurning, rejecting as insignificant. Names the inner act of treating as valueless. - **H0937 buz** — contempt as experienced; most present in contexts where contempt is received. The most socially-relational of the terms. - **H0963 biz.za.von** — contempt as social contagion; one act spreading the disposition through … |
| 114 | M06-B | T1.1.3 | Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry… | finding | Strongly directional: almost universally directed upward (creature toward Creator, covenant, sacred things) or received downward (from nations toward Israel). Constitutional: Psa 10:13 — "say in his heart" — the heart is the constitutional location. |
| 122 | M06-B | T1.2.1 | What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, … | finding | Primarily an **inner act of evaluation** — the heart assigning low worth to what deserves high worth. | 130 | M06-B | T1.2.2 | Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first … | finding | Has constituent elements: (1) perception of the object; (2) moral evaluation rendering a low-worth verdict; (3) volitional response — acting as if freed from the object's claim; (4) optional speech-expression of the inner verdict. |
| 138 | M06-B | T1.2.3 | What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutio… | finding | Contempt (M06-B) is an inner act and habit of the moral evaluation faculty in which the person assigns low or no worth to what is sacred, covenantally significant, or inherently worthy — constitutionally located in the heart, engaging the moral evaluation and cognitive faculties, producing a closed inner posture that excludes the despised object from its proper claim on the person's honour, compliance, or reverence. |
| 146 | M06-B | T1.3.1 | What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands agains… | finding | Honour and reverence (ka.vod, yir.ah). | 162 | M06-B | T1.3.3 | What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin? | finding | Not hatred: hatred is sustained relational enmity; contempt is a worth-evaluation that can be directed at things and words as much as persons. Not abhorrence (M06-C): abhorrence is visceral moral recoil; contempt is cool evaluative dismissal. Not anger: contempt can be cool and comfortable (Job 12:5 — "in the thought of one who is at ease"). Contempt is the cool verdict; abhorrence is the visceral recoil; hatred is the sustained enmity. |
| 170 | M06-B | T1.4.1 | In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person? | finding | Four modes: (1) **Inner speech mode** — the contempt is articulated privately (Psa 10:13: "say in his heart, 'You will not call to account'"). (3) **Speech-act mode** — ne.a.tsah: blasphemous contempt expressed outward in speech (Neh 9:18, 9:26, Eze 35:12). (4) **Contagion mode** — biz.za.von: contempt spreading from one person's act through a community (Est 1:18). |

When operating at the national level (groups 1281-001, 1277-001): contempt functions as collective inner disposition toward another people. |

It is not a wrong act of moral evaluation but the absence of positive moral qualities (mercy, fear of God, restraint). |
| 276 | M06-D | T1.8.2 | What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification? | finding | Pro 12:10: "the mercy of the wicked is cruel" — character-level operation pervasive through all acts. Psa 54:3's "do not set God before themselves" names the absent character quality. |
| 284 | M06-D | T1.8.3 | Does this characteristic carry secondary dimensions — and if so, what are they, and do they compete… | finding | Secondary: **Agency/Power** (cruelty operates through the exercise of power; ruthlessness is the disposition of those with power acting without divine constraint). **Emotion — Negative** (in the recipient register — group 1775-002 — fear, distress, longing for deliverance). |
| 101 | M06-E | T1.1.1 | What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about it… | finding | Called "Reproach." The name signals a received condition — something done to the person, not something the person does. It carries both the social (public) and the inner (felt) dimensions: reproach is not merely an external label but an inner experience that shapes the whole person's way of being in the world. |
| 109 | M06-E | T1.1.2 | What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical… | finding | Five terms covering different registers: - **H2781 cher.pah** — the dominant term (72 verses); reproach, disgrace, taunt, insult. Operates across individual and corporate levels with the same word — from "reproaches have broken my heart" (Psa 69:20) to "I will make you a desolation and an object of reproach among the nations" (Eze 5:14). - **H5007A ne.a.tsah** — disgrace as an inner condition in extremity (2Ki 19:3); the acute overwhelming shame-experience when crisis produces incapacity. - **H8146 sa.ni** — the disfavoured/unloved; chronic relational condition of being the less-loved spouse … |
| 117 | M06-E | T1.1.3 | Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry… | finding | Directional: entirely received — all five terms name what is done to the person, not what the person holds. Constitutional: Psa 69:20 — "reproaches have broken my heart"; Psa 89:50 — "I bear in my heart the insults." The heart is the location where reproach is carried and felt. |
| 125 | M06-E | T1.2.1 | What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, … | finding | An **inner-social condition** — a state the person inhabits with both outer social (being publicly regarded as shameful) and inner (feeling the weight) dimensions. It is neither a disposition (held by the agent) nor an act (performed) but a condition in the full sense — a state of being in which the person exists. |
| 133 | M06-E | T1.2.2 | Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first … | finding | Has constituent elements: (1) social assignment by others (designation as shameful); (2) inner reception (the person feels the weight); (3) relational isolation produced by the condition; (4) identity pressure as the designation challenges who the person is. |
| 141 | M06-E | T1.2.3 | What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutio… | finding | Reproach (M06-E) is the inner-social condition of being publicly designated as an object of shame and contempt — received from outside but felt and borne at the level of the heart and personal identity. It is constitutional in that it shapes the whole person's way of existing in the world, yet its source is in the disordered inner lives of those who hate, despise, and mistreat. Its eschatological trajectory is complete removal and the restoration of honour. |
| 149 | M06-E | T1.3.1 | What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands agains… | finding | Honour and vindication (ka.vod). Isa 60:15: "whereas you have been forsaken and hated...I will make you majestic forever, a joy from age to age." The structural opposite is not merely the absence of reproach but the positive attribution of honour, dignity, and public recognition of worth. |
| 157 | M06-E | T1.3.2 | What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist? | finding | Honour (Mal 2:9 — the opposite of reproach). Confident public presence (Psa 31:11 — "those who see me in the street flee from me"). Pro 6:33: "his disgrace will not be wiped away" — the most severe reproach excludes even the possibility of restoration without divine intervention. |
| 165 | M06-E | T1.3.3 | What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin? | finding | Not guilt: guilt is an inner conviction of wrongdoing; reproach is the social-inner condition that may or may not correspond to actual wrongdoing. Many verses document unjust reproach (Psa 69:4: "those who hate me without cause"). Not shame: shame can be an inner state independent of social designation; reproach is specifically the social-outer designation felt inwardly. They often co-occur (Psa 69:19: "my reproach, and my shame and my dishonor") but are distinguishable. |
| 173 | M06-E | T1.4.1 | In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person? | finding | Four modes: (1) **Personal acute mode** — ne.a.tsah: the overwhelming shame-experience in a moment of crisis (2Ki 19:3). (2) **Personal chronic mode** — cher.pah (individual): the sustained inner-social condition of the person publicly designated as shameful (Psa 69:7). (3) **Corporate-covenantal mode** — cher.pah (communal): reproach as the condition of a whole people among the nations (Eze 5:14). (4) **Dignity-violation mode** — hubrizō: reproach as the experience of having one's dignity actively stripped (1Th 2:2). |
| 181 | M06-E | T1.4.2 | Does this characteristic operate differently depending on context, direction, or constitutional lev… | finding | Yes — varies by duration (acute vs chronic), scope (individual vs communal), and source (active dignity-stripping vs passive social designation). The inner experience varies correspondingly. |
| 189 | M06-E | T1.4.3 | Does this characteristic have a communicative or speech-based mode of operation — and if so, how do… | finding | Yes, extensively. Reproach is characteristically communicated through speech: the taunt, the mockery, the insult. Lam 3:61: "you have heard their taunts, O Lord." Psa 79:12: "the taunts with which they have taunted you." Jer 20:8: "the word of the Lord has become for me a reproach and derision all day long." Speech is the primary vehicle through which reproach is delivered and maintained. |
| 197 | M06-E | T1.5.1 | What is the first or most immediate inner-being response to receiving or encountering this characte… | finding | Psa 69:20: "reproaches have broken my heart, so that I am in despair." The immediate response is heart-breaking — a shattering of the inner self. The secondary immediate response: "I looked for pity, but there was none, and for comforters, but I found none" — the reaching out for relational support, and the compounding of the condition when that support is absent. |

| 245 | M06-E | T1.7.1 | What inner conditions or orientations enable genuine reception of this characteristic? | finding | Social isolation (Psa 69:20b — "I looked for pity, but there was none") — when the reproached person has no relational resource, reproach takes hold most completely. The inner state involves the pain of reproach alongside a groundedness in God's regard that keeps the reproach from colonising the person's identity. |
| 269 | M06-E | T1.8.1 | What is the primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic from the programme's dimension voc… | finding | **Emotion — Negative.** Reproach is primarily an affective-experiential condition — the felt inner state of being publicly shamed. | 277 | M06-E | T1.8.2 | What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification? | finding | "Broken my heart" (Psa 69:20), "in despair," "I am a worm and not a man" (Psa 22:6), "great trouble and shame" (Neh 1:3) — consistently affective vocabulary throughout M06-E. |

| 102 | M06-F | T1.1.1 | What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about it… | finding | Called "Hostility/Enmity." The double label signals a relational structure of structured opposition — the adversary relationship — rather than a purely inner disposition. It covers both the structural form (enmity as position) and the inner quality of the relationship (hostility as orientation). |
| 110 | M06-F | T1.1.2 | What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical… | finding | Four terms naming relational positions: - **H3401 ya.riv** — adversary/one who contends; legal contention register. Always in the appeal context (Psa 35:1, Isa 49:25, Jer 18:19). - **G0476 antidikos** — adversary/opponent/accuser; the Greek legal term for the opposing party. Spans legal (Mat 5:25, Luk 12:58), human-appeal (Luk 18:3), and cosmic (1Pe 5:8 — the devil) registers. - **H0340 a.yav** — enemy, to be hostile; one verse (Exo 23:22) where God adopts this posture. - **H7009 qim** — adversary as the object of divine judgment; one verse (Job 22:20); the inner response is relief. |
| 118 | M06-F | T1.1.3 | Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry… | finding | Constitutively relational — the adversary relationship always involves two parties in structured opposition. Unlike other M06 sub-groups which name one party's inner disposition, M06-F names the relational structure between parties. The inner-being content is generated by the structure in the one who faces it. |
| 126 | M06-F | T1.2.1 | What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, … | finding | A **relational category that generates inner-being responses** — the adversary relationship names a position or role in a relational structure, not an inner quality of the person occupying that position. | 134 | M06-F | T1.2.2 | Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first … | finding | Has constituent elements generated by the relational structure: (1) the adversarial positioning of another party; (2) the urgency and appeal it generates; (3) the vigilance it commands; (4) the relief when it is removed. |
| 142 | M06-F | T1.2.3 | What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutio… | finding | Hostility/Enmity (M06-F) is a relational structure of structured opposition — the adversary relationship — that generates characteristic inner-being responses in the person who faces it: appeal, urgency, vigilance, and relief. The inner-being content resides in the person facing the adversary (not in the adversary's own inner state), and the appropriate response is grounded in relationship with God as the one who contends for the person. |
| 150 | M06-F | T1.3.1 | What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands agains… | finding | Advocacy and covenant companionship — God contending for rather than against. The structural opposite of facing an adversary is having an advocate who contends on one's behalf. |
| 158 | M06-F | T1.3.2 | What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist? | finding | The adversary relationship generates urgency and appeal — it resists passivity and complacency. 1Pe 5:8: "be sober-minded; be watchful" — the characteristic demands active vigilance. |
| 166 | M06-F | T1.3.3 | What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin? | finding | Not hatred (M06-A): hatred is an inner disposition of the agent; the adversary relationship is a relational structure. Not cruelty (M06-D): the ruthless person is characterised by an inner character void; the adversary person occupies a relational position that may or may not involve the inner character qualities of the other sub-groups. An adversary can be just (God in Exo 23:22) or unjust (the devil in 1Pe 5:8). |
| 174 | M06-F | T1.4.1 | In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person? | finding | Three modes: (1) **Legal/judicial mode** — antidikos: the adversary in a legal dispute generating urgency (Mat 5:25, Luk 12:58, 18:3). The same structural form generates very different inner-being responses depending on who the adversary is and what is at stake. |

| 103 | M06-G | T1.1.1 | What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about it… | finding | Called "Malice." The name signals a compound inner state — not a single emotion but the simultaneous operation of enmity, contempt, and active pleasure in another's destruction. The name carries the sense of something specifically and deliberately harmful, not merely hostile. |
| 111 | M06-G | T1.1.2 | What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical… | finding | One term: - **H7589 she.at** — malice, spite, wickedness. Its rarity is consistent with its specificity: malice as a compound is a recognisable inner-being reality requiring its own term because it is not reducible to its constituent parts. |
| 119 | M06-G | T1.1.3 | Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry… | finding | Soul-constitutional: Eze 25:6 explicitly names the soul as the location ("malice within your soul"). Directional: outward toward another's destruction. Relational: always directed at a specific other whose suffering provides the occasion for the soul's pleasure. --- ### T1.2 — Kind |
| 127 | M06-G | T1.2.1 | What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, … | finding | A **compound disposition** — constituted by the simultaneous operation of sustained enmity, contempt for the other's worth, and active pleasure in their destruction. It is not a simple emotion but a compound that produces something qualitatively different from its constituent parts. |
| 135 | M06-G | T1.2.2 | Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first … | finding | Compound with three constituent elements: (1) sustained enmity (M06-A elements); (2) contempt for the other's worth (M06-B elements); (3) active pleasure in the other's destruction (the unique additional element that distinguishes malice from its constituents). All three must be simultaneously present for malice to be present in its full form. |
| 143 | M06-G | T1.2.3 | What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutio… | finding | Malice (M06-G) is a compound inner disposition of the soul — the simultaneous operation of sustained enmity, contempt for the other's worth, and active pleasure in their destruction — explicitly located in the soul (nephesh), producing bodily expression (clapping, stamping), and characterised by self-perpetuating intensity (the pleasure renews itself in the continued destruction of the other). --- ### T1.3 — Boundary |
| 151 | M06-G | T1.3.1 | What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands agains… | finding | Compassion — the inner disposition that takes pain in another's suffering rather than pleasure. The compound opposite: solidarity rather than enmity, recognition of worth rather than contempt, sorrow at harm rather than joy. |
| 159 | M06-G | T1.3.2 | What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist? | finding | Compassion for the other's suffering. Recognition of the other as a person whose suffering matters. Any restraint on the pleasure taken in their destruction. |
| 167 | M06-G | T1.3.3 | What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin? | finding | Not hatred alone: hatred does not necessarily take pleasure in harm; malice specifically does. Not contempt alone: contempt dismisses; malice celebrates destruction. Not cruelty: cruelty is the character void (absence of mercy); malice is the positive presence of pleasure in harm. All three can co-occur but they are distinct inner-being realities. --- ### T1.4 — Modes of Operation |
| 175 | M06-G | T1.4.1 | In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person? | finding | One mode with three distinctive features: **Soul-level pleasure in destruction** as the primary mode (Eze 25:6: "rejoiced with all the malice within your soul"); **purposive destruction** driving actively toward harm (Eze 25:15: "to destroy in never-ending enmity"); **contempt-compound** where joy and contempt are simultaneous and inseparable (Eze 36:5: "wholehearted joy and utter contempt"). |
| 183 | M06-G | T1.4.2 | Does this characteristic operate differently depending on context, direction, or constitutional lev… | finding | Limited variation — all three verses (Eze 25:6, 25:15, 36:5) show the same mode across different nations (Ammon, Philistia, Edom). The characteristic appears structurally consistent across national contexts. |
| 191 | M06-G | T1.4.3 | Does this characteristic have a communicative or speech-based mode of operation — and if so, how do… | finding | No speech mode evidenced. The characteristic expresses through bodily action (Eze 25:6: clapping hands, stamping feet) rather than through speech. Silent on speech mode. --- ### T1.5 — Immediate Response |
| 199 | M06-G | T1.5.1 | What is the first or most immediate inner-being response to receiving or encountering this characte… | finding | Bodily expression: Eze 25:6 — "you have clapped your hands and stamped your feet." The inner soul-level pleasure immediately expresses through physical celebration — the most somatic immediate expression in M06. |
| 207 | M06-G | T1.5.2 | Is the immediate response consistent across the verse evidence, or does it vary? | finding | Three verses — consistency is limited by the thin corpus. The bodily expression is consistent across all three (the nations celebrate Israel's suffering). Whether the immediate internal pleasure-of-malice onset is consistent is not described phenomenologically. |
| 215 | M06-G | T1.5.3 | Where the verse evidence is silent on immediate response, note this explicitly. | finding | The phenomenology of malice-onset — what it feels like as the soul begins to rejoice in another's destruction — is not described. --- ### T1.6 — Sustained Effect |

The sustained effect is a deepening inner orientation toward the other's destruction as the source of the soul's pleasure — the characteristic does not cycle down but intensifies. |
| 231 | M06-G | T1.6.2 | What states, qualities, capacities, or orientations does sustained exposure to or possession of thi… | finding | The "never-ending" quality of malice (Eze 25:15) names the self-sustaining inner orientation: the soul finds its pleasure in the object's destruction and seeks more occasions for that pleasure. |
| 239 | M06-G | T1.6.3 | Does the sustained effect differ from the immediate response — and if so, how? | finding | [Sub-group not separately addressed in source — see cluster-level finding (cluster_subgroup_id IS NULL) for this prompt] |
| 247 | M06-G | T1.7.1 | What inner conditions or orientations enable genuine reception of this characteristic? | finding | Sustained enmity toward another, combined with the loss of regard for their worth (contempt), and the progressive acquisition of a twisted inner pleasure from the reversal of their fortunes. All three constituent elements must be simultaneously present. |
| 255 | M06-G | T1.7.2 | What inner conditions block, distort, or prevent reception? | finding | Compassion for the other — the genuine capacity to be pained by another's suffering blocks the development of pleasure in it. | 263 | M06-G | T1.7.3 | What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic but does not receive… | finding | The verse evidence does not describe the inner state of those who are the objects of malice (Israel). | 271 | M06-G | T1.8.1 | What is the primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic from the programme's dimension voc… | finding | **Emotion — Negative** (compound). Uniquely, M06-G's primary dimension is also constitutively relational and moral — the three dimensions are co-constituent rather than primary and secondary. |
| 279 | M06-G | T1.8.2 | What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification? | finding | Eze 25:6: "rejoiced with all the malice within your soul"; Eze 36:5: "wholehearted joy" — the affective content (joy, pleasure) is primary. |
| 287 | M06-G | T1.8.3 | Does this characteristic carry secondary dimensions — and if so, what are they, and do they compete… | finding | The three dimensions (Emotion — Negative, Relational Disposition, Moral Character) are co-constituent rather than primary and secondary. M06-G is the only sub-group where multiple dimensions are constitutive of the characteristic rather than one primary and others secondary. --- *End of T0 and T1 — continuing in next section* |
### Tier T2

| # | Sub-group | Q-code | Q-text | Status | Finding |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| 296 | — | T2.1.1 | Is this characteristic explicitly located at the spirit level in the verse evidence? | cluster_synthesis | No verse in any M06 sub-group explicitly locates any M06 characteristic at the spirit (ruach) level. The spirit is entirely silent across the cluster. Mal 2:16 contains "guard yourselves in your spirit" in a verse adjacent to marital faithlessness, but the command is to guard the spirit against faithlessness — not to locate the characteristic of hatred or contempt in the spirit. This is a cluster-level silence. |
| 304 | — | T2.1.2 | Does the evidence indicate that this characteristic originates in or is primarily a spirit-level ph… | cluster_synthesis | Not evidenced for any sub-group. The verse evidence locates M06 characteristics in the heart (levav) and soul (nephesh), not in the spirit (ruach). The spirit-level is not named as the originating location of hatred, contempt, abhorrence, cruelty, reproach, enmity, or malice in any verse across the cluster. |
| 312 | — | T2.1.3 | What does spirit-level location reveal about the nature and depth of this characteristic? | silent | Cannot be answered — spirit-level location is not evidenced for any sub-group. |
| 320 | — | T2.1.4 | If the evidence is silent on spirit-level location, note this explicitly. | cluster_synthesis | Spirit-level location is silent across the entire M06 cluster. None of the 429 connected verse rows across the cluster's seven sub-groups locates a M06 characteristic at the ruach level. This silence is itself a finding: the cluster of characteristics named by M06 operates below the spirit level — in the heart and soul registers — without penetrating to or originating from the spirit. --- ### T2.2 — Soul-Level Location |

| 514 | M06-B | T2.9.1 | Where does this characteristic originate constitutionally — is it generated from within the person,… | finding | Two enabling origins: (1) **Pride and comfort** — Job 12:5: "in the thought of one who is at ease there is contempt for misfortune"; Psa 123:4: "the contempt of the proud" — the person's comfortable superiority generates contempt. | 587 | M06-C | T3.11.1 | Does this characteristic engage the relational capacity — the constitutional equipment for genuine … | finding | Abhorrence destroys the relational capacity toward the object completely through social abandonment. This is more total than hatred (which at least maintains the hostile relational connection) and more total than contempt (which dismisses but does not necessarily avoid all contact). Abhorrence produces complete relational withdrawal. |

| 801 | M06-A | T4.1.1 | Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating from God toward the human person — and i… | finding | Yes. | 809 | M06-A | T4.1.2 | What does the evidence reveal about the basis on which God extends this characteristic — is it cond… | finding | God's hatred is consistently responsive and morally grounded — never arbitrary. Every instance of divine hatred in M06-A has an explicit moral grounding. |
| 817 | M06-A | T4.1.3 | What does God's extension of this characteristic reveal about his disposition toward the human pers… | finding | God's hatred of evil and of those who persistently choose it reveals the depth and seriousness of his moral commitments. He is not indifferent to wickedness — his inner being (nephesh) is set against it. This means that when God acts in judgment against evil, he is not merely applying an external rule but expressing an inner disposition. Psa 5:5-6 shows the dual face: God hates evildoers and abhors the bloodthirsty — this is paired with God's grace toward those who seek him (v.7). The hatred and the welcome exist in the same God, directed toward different objects. |

These are opposite expressions of the same relational orientation toward God. |

| 865 | M06-A | T4.3.1 | Does the verse evidence show this characteristic being extended by one person toward another — and … | finding | Yes, extensively in groups 1601 and 1200. Pro 26:24 — the hater gives hatred through disguised speech. The giving of person-directed hatred is not typically a discrete act but an ongoing orientation. |
| 873 | M06-A | T4.3.2 | What inner conditions or orientations in the giver enable genuine extension of this characteristic? | finding | An unresolved perceived injury, loss, or violation that has not been covered (Pro 10:12: love covers offences; hatred keeps them open). | 993 | M06-A | T5.1.1 | Does this characteristic produce transformation in the person — and if so, does it change the perso… | finding | Both. In the disordered register: hatred transforms the person's condition by progressively narrowing the inner life around the object of hatred. Gen 37:4-8 shows the transformation unfolding in steps — each provocation deepens the hatred ("they hated him even more"), and what begins as a response to perceived favouritism settles into an inner organising principle that makes peaceful speech impossible and leads eventually to the decision to sell Joseph into slavery. The person has not merely acquired an attitude; they have been reorganised differently inside. In the righteous register: hatred… |
| 1001 | M06-A | T5.1.2 | Is the transformation produced by this characteristic reversible or irreversible in the verse evide… | finding | In the disordered register: no verse in M06-A describes a person moving from sustained hatred of a person to love or reconciliation using these terms. The transformation hatred produces appears highly resistant to reversal. Eschatologically: Psa 81:15 shows that the person whose defining inner orientation is hatred of God will "cringe toward him" at the final horizon — that is exposure and collapse, not reconciliation. The fate "lasts forever." |

Something is perceived as injury or injustice. 2. **Inner settlement** — Gen 37:4: "they hated him." The perception has produced a settled inner state. 3. **Surface manifestation** — Gen 37:4: "they could not speak peacefully to him." The inner state affects outward behaviour before any deliberate act. 4. **Intensification** — Gen 37:5, 8: "they hated him even more." Each new provocation deepens the … |

| 1041 | M06-A | T5.3.1 | What mechanism does this characteristic use to produce change in the person — discipline, encounter… | finding | Progressive inner organisation around the object of hatred. The characteristic works by recruiting each inner faculty toward the hostile orientation: perception is narrowed to register injury-evidence, memory preserves the wound, cognition is recruited into concealment, imagination generates plans of harm, volition is organised toward a hostile end. The Joseph narrative shows this across time — the brothers' inner lives become increasingly organised around their hatred of Joseph. |
| 1049 | M06-A | T5.3.2 | Does the evidence distinguish between mechanisms in different contexts? | finding | In the person-directed register: largely self-sustaining through the person's own perception and memory. In the communal register (group 1199): socially self-reinforcing — communal hatred maintains itself across the community. In Psa 105:25: externally introduced by divine action — the mechanism can come from outside the person. |
| 1057 | M06-A | T5.3.3 | If the evidence is silent on mechanism, note this explicitly. | finding | [Sub-group not separately addressed in source — see cluster-level finding (cluster_subgroup_id IS NULL) for this prompt] |
| 1065 | M06-A | T5.4.1 | Does the verse evidence show this characteristic operating in relation to suffering or affliction —… | finding | Extensively. Hatred is one of the primary causes of suffering in the M06-A corpus. The Psalms of lament are suffused with the experience of being hated: Psa 69:4 ("those who hate me without cause"), Psa 25:19 ("many are those who hate me"), Psa 38:19 ("many are those who hate me wrongfully"). In the Job material: Job 16:9 names God as the perceived source of persistent hostile treatment — "he has torn me in his wrath and hated me" — the experience of suffering can produce the perception of being hated even when that is not the cause. |
| 1073 | M06-A | T5.4.2 | Does suffering deepen, test, reveal, or produce this characteristic in the person? | finding | Suffering precipitates hatred where an underlying wound is not resolved: 2Sa 13:22 — Absalom's sustained hatred of Amnon, rooted in the suffering of Tamar's violation. | 1353 | M06-A | T7.1.1 | What are the primary Hebrew and Greek terms for this characteristic — and what do their root meanin… | finding | H8130 sa.ne (verb — to hate; sustained hostility, the most attested), H8135 sin.ah (noun — hatred as condition, causal force), H7852 sa.tam (grudge-bearing persistence), H4895 mas.te.mah (communal hostile atmosphere), H8131 se.na (Aramaic person-type designation). | 1425 | M06-A | T7.1.10 | What does the full vocabulary arc reveal about this characteristic's complete semantic range? | finding | The arc moves from the fundamental verb (sa.ne — to hate) through noun-quality (sin.ah — hatred as condition), persistence-form (sa.tam), communal-climate form (mas.te.mah), and person-type designation (se.na). This arc covers hatred from its most immediate form through its temporal and social dimensions to its eschatological person-type. The vocabulary has been differentiated by centuries of use to cover the full range of the characteristic's operation. |
| 1361 | M06-A | T7.1.2 | What is the grammatical range of the primary term (noun, verb, adjective, participle) — and what do… | finding | Active verb ("they hated him"), passive verb ("Leah was hated"), agent-noun person-type ("those who hate me"), infinitive construct in legal use ("without having hated him in the past"). Each grammatical form encodes a different aspect: the act, the condition, the person-type defined by the disposition, and the legally operative prior state. |
| 1369 | M06-A | T7.1.3 | What is the semantic range of the primary term — across what breadth of meaning does it operate? | finding | Covers six distinct registers across the 13 M06-A groups: relational enmity between persons, marital disfavour with legal consequence, commanded righteous orientation away from evil, divine sustained opposition to sin, divine response to God-haters, and the underlying category of "enemy/foe." The breadth is the widest of any primary term in M06. |
| 1377 | M06-A | T7.1.4 | Does the vocabulary include terms that distinguish distinct aspects of this characteristic — dispos… | finding | Yes, comprehensively. Sa.ne covers both act and condition. | 1417 | M06-A | T7.1.9 | Is there a term newly coined in the NT period for this characteristic — and if so, what does that c… | finding | [Sub-group not separately addressed in source — see cluster-level finding (cluster_subgroup_id IS NULL) for this prompt] |
| 1433 | M06-A | T7.2.1 | What is the function of this characteristic's primary term within its primary verse — what role doe… | finding | Sa.ne functions as the object of the prohibition: "you shall not hate your brother in your heart." The sentence structure is: prohibition (do not hate) + location (in your heart) + alternative command (reason frankly) + consequence (lest you incur sin). Sa.ne carries the full weight of the prohibition's object. By naming hatred in the heart, the verse makes the inner state — not the external act — the locus of the prohibition. The argument: inner hatred is morally culpable before it produces any act; the alternative is honest relational engagement, not merely behavioural restraint. |
| 1441 | M06-A | T7.2.2 | What literary form carries the primary verse evidence (narrative, psalm, wisdom, prophecy, epistle,… | finding | Five forms: legal text (Lev 19:17, Deu 19:4, 19:11, Num 35:20), narrative (Gen 37:4-8, Gen 27:41, 2Sa 13:15), Psalm/lament (Psa 25:19, 69:4, 109:3-5, 139:21-22), wisdom (Pro 10:12, 10:18, 26:24-26, 8:13), and prophetic (Isa 1:14, Amos 5:10-15, Hos 9:7-8). Each form requires distinct interpretive discipline: legal precision (the term operates as a legal criterion), narrative tracking (hatred develops through plot), lament (first-person testimony of receiving hatred), wisdom (aphoristic compression of observed patterns), and prophetic (divine address in covenantal context). |
| 1449 | M06-A | T7.2.3 | What is the logical structure of key arguments in the verse evidence — what are the premises and co… | finding | Inner state (hatred) + failure to confront honestly = moral culpability (sin). The premise: inner states are morally binding before they produce acts. The conclusion: the alternative to hatred is honest confrontation, not merely behavioural restraint. |
| 1457 | M06-A | T7.2.4 | What contextual setting carries the primary verse evidence (judicial, liturgical, covenantal, commu… | finding | Legal/judicial (Deu 19, Num 35 — hatred as legal criterion), covenantal (Exo 20:5 — hatred within the Decalogue), liturgical/Psalmic (Psa 139:21-22, Psa 97:10 — righteous hatred as worship-declaration), wisdom (Pro 8:13, 10:12), prophetic (Isa 1:14, Amos 5, Hos 9). |
| 1465 | M06-A | T7.2.5 | Does any verse function as the primary anchor for this characteristic — the verse that most fully a… | finding | Lev 19:17 — the only verse in M06-A that simultaneously names the constitutional location (heart), the moral culpability of the inner state before any act, the alternative (frank confrontation), and the consequence (incurring sin). It is the most definitionally complete verse for the whole sub-group. |
| 1473 | M06-A | T7.2.6 | What does the primary anchor verse reveal that no other verse reveals? | finding | Uniquely reveals: (1) heart as explicit constitutional location; (2) the alternative (frank confrontation) — what replaces hatred; (3) moral culpability of the inner state before any act. No other M06-A verse combines all three. |
| 1481 | M06-A | T7.3.1 | Which human science framework (psychology, moral philosophy, developmental psychology, sociology, a… | finding | Primary: clinical and social psychology — specifically the psychology of hatred, hostility, and aggression, and moral emotion research. Secondary: developmental psychology (formation of hatred and prejudice over time) and neuroscience (neural substrates of sustained hostile states). |
| 1489 | M06-A | T7.3.2 | Where the human science framework illuminates the verse evidence — making a finding more coherent o… | finding | Three convergences: (1) Clinical psychology distinguishes acute anger (episodic) from chronic hostility (dispositional, sustained) — mapping precisely onto the verse evidence's distinction between sa.ne (settled disposition) and momentary anger (a.phon). (2) Rumination — the repetitive mental rehearsal of perceived injury — is the mechanism sustaining chronic hostility; this maps onto sa.tam (grudge-bearing preserved in memory). (3) Developmental psychology and epigenetics document that hostile relational environments in childhood shape attachment patterns and inner-life architecture; this ma… |
| 1497 | M06-A | T7.3.3 | Where the verse evidence and the human science framework diverge, what does the divergence reveal? | finding | Clinical psychology has no category for morally required or virtuous hatred. All sustained hostile dispositions are treated as pathological or problems to be reduced. The verse evidence's positive space for righteous hatred (Pro 8:13: "the fear of the Lord is hatred of evil"; Psa 97:10: "hate evil!"; Amo 5:15: commanded hatred) is beyond the science's framework. This is a genuine divergence — not register divergence but a difference in what constitutes the properly ordered inner life. |
| 1505 | M06-A | T7.3.4 | Does the human science framework surface any aspect of this characteristic that the verse evidence … | finding | The science of prejudice and in-group/out-group hostility (social psychology) is highly developed, documenting how group identity shapes the direction and intensity of hostile dispositions. The verse evidence documents this phenomenon (Egypt hating Israel, surrounding nations hating Zion) but does not articulate the mechanism by which group-identity-based hatred operates at the inner-being level. The social psychology of prejudice would add mechanistic description to the communal-level hatred documented in groups 1199 and 3596. |

| 1487 | M06-G | T7.3.1 | Which human science framework (psychology, moral philosophy, developmental psychology, sociology, a… | finding | Primary: moral psychology and aggression research — specifically the psychology of Schadenfreude (pleasure in another's misfortune) and revenge. | 1495 | M06-G | T7.3.2 | Where the human science framework illuminates the verse evidence — making a finding more coherent o… | finding | Convergences: research on Schadenfreude documents that pleasure in another's suffering is a distinct psychological phenomenon, associated with prior feelings of inferiority or envy, and characterised by activation of reward pathways in the brain. Maps directly onto M06-G: the nations' malice combines prior enmity (inferiority/envy-adjacent) with the reward of seeing the powerful (Israel) brought low. The pleasure is neurologically real, not merely behavioural. |
| 1503 | M06-G | T7.3.3 | Where the verse evidence and the human science framework diverge, what does the divergence reveal? | finding | "Never-ending enmity" (Eze 25:15) names a total and perpetual soul-orientation toward another's destruction that goes beyond the science's situational Schadenfreude category. The science documents situational pleasure in misfortune; the verse evidence names a constitutive soul-orientation of sustained destructive pleasure. |

## 3. Terms — meaning detail

### Sub-group M06-A — Hatred

#### H4895 · *mas.te.mah* (Hebrew) — hatred

- mti_id: `903`  ·  md_version: `1`  ·  testament: `OT_only`  ·  occurrences: `2`

**Senses:**

- `1` animosity, enmity


#### H7852 · *sa.tam* (Hebrew) — to hate

- mti_id: `5518`  ·  md_version: `1`  ·  testament: `OT_only`  ·  occurrences: `6`

**Senses:**

- `1` to hate, oppose oneself to, bear a grudge, retain animosity against, cherish animosity against
  - `1a` (Qal) to cherish animosity against


#### H8130 · *sa.ne* (Hebrew) — to hate

- mti_id: `550`  ·  md_version: `1`  ·  testament: `OT_only`  ·  occurrences: `about 156`

**Senses:**

- `1` to hate, be hateful
  - `1a` (Qal) to hate
  - `1a1` of man
  - `1a2` of God
  - `1a3` hater, one hating, enemy (participle) (subst)
  - `1b` (Niphal) to be hated
  - `1c` (Piel) hater (participle)
  - `1c1` of persons, nations, God, wisdom Aramaic equivalent: se.ne (שְׂנָא "to hate" H8131)
